Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="white-space:nowrap;text-align:right;">1939\xe2\x80\x931945</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><b>See also</b></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Political history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Political history (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Social history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Social history (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_English_history" title="Timeline of English history">Timeline</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template:Periods in English history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template talk:Periods in English history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Periods_in_English_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>England in the High Middle Ages</b> includes the history of England between the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman Conquest</a> in 1066 and the death of <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">King John</a>, considered by some to be the last of the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin kings of England</a>, in 1216. A disputed succession and victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">Battle of Hastings</a> led to the conquest of England by <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William of Normandy</a> in 1066. This linked the crown of England with possessions in France and brought a new aristocracy to the country that dominated landholding, government and the church. They brought with them the French language and maintained their rule through a system of castles and the introduction of a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> system of landholding. By the time of William\'s death in 1087, England formed the largest part of an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">Anglo-Norman</a> empire, ruled by nobles with landholdings across England, Normandy and Wales. William\'s sons disputed succession to his lands, with <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II</a> emerging as ruler of England and much of Normandy. On his death in 1100 his younger brother claimed the throne as <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I</a> and defeated his brother <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert</a> to reunite England and Normandy. Henry was a ruthless yet effective king, but after the death of his only male heir in the <a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a> tragedy, he persuaded his barons to recognise his daughter <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Matilda</a> as heir. When Henry died in 1135 her cousin <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen of Blois</a> had himself proclaimed king, leading to a civil war known as <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">The Anarchy</a>. Eventually Stephen recognised Matilda\'s son <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry</a> as his heir and when Stephen died in 1154, he succeeded as Henry II.\n</p><p>Henry, considered the first Angevin king of England, had extensive holdings in France and asserted his authority over Wales, Scotland and Ireland. He clashed with his appointee to the Archbishopric of Canterbury <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a>, resulting in Becket\'s murder and Henry making a dramatic exhibition of penance. The later part of his reign was dominated by rebellions involving his sons and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II of France</a> that forced him to accept his son <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard</a> as sole heir. Richard acceded to the Angevin inheritance on Henry\'s death in 1189 and almost immediately departed on a <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Crusade</a>. On his return journey he was taken hostage in Germany and a huge ransom was paid in order to secure his release in 1194. He spent the remainder of his reign restoring his lands in France, dying in 1199. His younger brother John succeeded in England, and fought a successful war against Richard\'s nephew <a href="/wiki/Arthur_I,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Arthur I, Duke of Brittany">Arthur</a> for control of the French possession. John\'s behaviour led to rebellions by the Norman and Angevin barons that broke his control of the continental possessions. His attempt to retake Normandy and Anjou was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bouvines" title="Battle of Bouvines">Battle of Bouvines</a>. This weakened his position in England, eventually resulting in the treaty called <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, which limited royal power, and the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons&#39; War">First Barons\' War</a>. His death in 1216 is considered by some historians to mark the end of the Angevin period and the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Plantagenet_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantagenet dynasty">Plantagenet dynasty</a>.\n</p><p>The Normans adopted many <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> governmental institutions, but the feudal system concentrated more power in the hands of the king and a small elite. The rights and roles of women became more sharply defined. Noblewomen remained significant cultural and religious patrons and played an important part in political and military events. During the twelfth century divisions between conquerors and the English began to dissolve and they began to consider themselves superior to their Celtic neighbours. The conquest brought Norman and French churchmen to power. New reformed religious and military orders were introduced into England. By the early thirteenth century the church had largely won its argument for independence from the state, answering almost entirely to Rome. <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimages</a> were a popular religious practice and accumulating <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> became important for ambitious institutions. England played a prominent part in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second</a>, <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">Fifth Crusades</a>.\n</p><p>Between the ninth and thirteenth centuries England went through the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>, a prolonged period of warmer temperatures that allowed poorer land to be brought into cultivation. Agricultural land became typically organised around <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">manors</a>. By the eleventh century, a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> was flourishing across much of England, while the eastern and southern towns were heavily involved in international trade. Many hundreds of new towns, some of them <a href="/wiki/Planned_community" title="Planned community">planned communities</a>, were built, supporting the creation of <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charter_fair" title="Charter fair">charter fairs</a>. Anglo-Norman warfare was characterised by <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attritional</a> military campaigns of raids and seizure of castles. Naval forces enabled the transportation of troops and supplies, raids into hostile territory and attacks on enemy fleets. After the conquest the Normans built timber <a href="/wiki/Motte_and_bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte and bailey">motte and bailey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ringwork" title="Ringwork">ringwork</a> castles in large numbers, which were replaced by stone buildings from the twelfth century. The period has been used in a wide range of popular culture, including <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s plays. <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>\'s location of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> in the reign of Richard I and his emphasis on the conflict between Saxons and Normans set the template for later fiction and film adaptations.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" class="toctext">Warfare</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Art"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Art</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Literature_and_music"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Literature and music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Architecture"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Architecture</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Popular_representations"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Popular representations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_history">Political history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Political history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Normans">Normans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Normans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Normandy" title="House of Normandy">House of Normandy</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Norman England" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Norman_England_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Norman England (disambiguation)">Norman England (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_conquest">Norman conquest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Norman conquest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman conquest">Norman conquest</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg/220px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg/330px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg/440px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2948" data-file-height="2568" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Section of the Bayeux Tapestry showing the final stages of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">battle of Hastings</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1002 King <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_the_Unready" title="\xc3\x86thelred the Unready">\xc3\x86thelred II of England</a> married <a href="/wiki/Emma_of_Normandy" title="Emma of Normandy">Emma</a>, the sister of  <a href="/wiki/Richard_II,_Duke_of_Normandy" title="Richard II, Duke of Normandy">Richard II, Duke of Normandy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Unready54_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unready54-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Their son <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, who spent many years in exile in Normandy, succeeded to the English throne in 1042.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruling3_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruling3-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> This led to the establishment of a powerful Norman interest in English politics, as Edward drew heavily on his former hosts for support, bringing in Norman courtiers, soldiers, and clerics and appointing them to positions of power, particularly in the Church. Childless and embroiled in conflict with the formidable <a href="/wiki/Godwin,_Earl_of_Wessex" title="Godwin, Earl of Wessex">Godwin, Earl of Wessex</a>, and his sons, Edward may also have encouraged <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">Duke William of Normandy\'s</a> ambitions for the English throne.<sup id="cite_ref-Unification86_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unification86-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When King Edward died at the beginning of 1066, the lack of a clear heir led to a disputed succession in which several contenders laid claim to the throne of England.<sup id="cite_ref-Higham2000pp167-81_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham2000pp167-81-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Edward\'s immediate successor was the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Wessex" title="Earl of Wessex">Earl of Wessex</a>, Harold Godwinson, the richest and most powerful of the English aristocrats. Harold was elected king by the <a href="/wiki/Witenagemot" title="Witenagemot">Witenagemot</a> of England and crowned by <a href="/wiki/Ealdred_(archbishop_of_York)" title="Ealdred (archbishop of York)">the Archbishop of York, Ealdred</a>, although Norman propaganda claimed the ceremony was performed by <a href="/wiki/Stigand" title="Stigand">Stigand</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">uncanonically</a> elected <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Higham2000pp167-81_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham2000pp167-81-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Walker136_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker136-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Harold was immediately challenged by two powerful neighbouring rulers. Duke William claimed that he had been promised the throne by King Edward and that Harold had sworn agreement to this;<sup id="cite_ref-Bates2001pp73-77_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bates2001pp73-77-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> King Harald III of Norway, commonly known as Harald Hardrada, also contested the succession. His claim to the throne was based on an agreement between his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Magnus_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus I of Norway">Magnus I of Norway</a> and the earlier English king, <a href="/wiki/Harthacnut" title="Harthacnut">Harthacnut</a>, whereby if either died without heir, the other would inherit both England and Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-Higham2000pp188-90_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Higham2000pp188-90-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> William and Harald at once set about assembling troops and ships to invade England.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruling12_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruling12-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tostig_Godwinson" title="Tostig Godwinson">Tostig Godwinson</a>, brother of Harold, made a series of attacks in the north of England in early 1066 that may have been the beginning of a bid for the throne, but after defeat at the hands of <a href="/wiki/Edwin,_Earl_of_Mercia" title="Edwin, Earl of Mercia">Edwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morcar_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Morcar of Northumbria">Morcar</a> and the desertion of most of his followers he threw his lot in with Harald Hardrada, who invaded northern England in early September.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas33_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas33-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Harold defeated and killed Hardrada and Tostig at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stamford_Bridge" title="Battle of Stamford Bridge">battle of Stamford Bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harold158_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harold158-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> William invaded with an army of <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman followers</a> and mercenaries. Harold marched south to meet him, but was defeated and killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">battle of Hastings</a> on 14 October and William\'s forces rapidly occupied the south of England.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="William_I_.281066.E2.80.9387.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="William_I_(1066\xe2\x80\x9387)">William I (1066\xe2\x80\x9387)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: William I (1066\xe2\x80\x9387)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William I of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Londres_297..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Londres_297..jpg/170px-Londres_297..jpg" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Londres_297..jpg/255px-Londres_297..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Londres_297..jpg/340px-Londres_297..jpg 2x" data-file-width="644" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Londres_297..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Tower of London, originally begun by William the Conqueror to control London<sup id="cite_ref-Douglas216_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Douglas216-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_England_1086.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Map_of_England_1086.png/170px-Map_of_England_1086.png" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Map_of_England_1086.png/255px-Map_of_England_1086.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Map_of_England_1086.png/340px-Map_of_England_1086.png 2x" data-file-width="2398" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_England_1086.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>England in 1086 during the Domesday Survey</div></div></div>\n<p>Major revolts followed, which William suppressed before intervening in the north-east of England, establishing Norman control of York and <a href="/wiki/Harrying_of_the_North" title="Harrying of the North">devastating the region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Once England had been conquered, the Normans faced many challenges in maintaining control.<sup id="cite_ref-Stafford102_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stafford102-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> They were few in number compared to the native English population; including those from other parts of France, historians estimate the number of Norman settlers at around 8,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Mastery82_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mastery82-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> William\'s followers expected and received lands and titles in return for their service in the invasion,<sup id="cite_ref-Mastery79_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mastery79-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> but William claimed ultimate possession of the land in England over which his armies had given him <i>de facto</i> control, and asserted the right to dispose of it as he saw fit.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter2004p84_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter2004p84-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Henceforth, all land was "held" directly from the king in <a href="/wiki/Feudal_land_tenure" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal land tenure">feudal tenure</a> in return for military service.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter2004p84_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter2004p84-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> A Norman lord typically had properties located in a piecemeal fashion throughout England and Normandy, and not in a single geographic block.<sup id="cite_ref-Mastery83_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mastery83-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To find the lands to compensate his Norman followers, William initially confiscated the estates of all the English lords who had fought and died with Harold and redistributed part of their lands.<sup id="cite_ref-Mastery75_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mastery75-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> These confiscations led to revolts, which resulted in more confiscations, a cycle that continued for five years after the Battle of Hastings.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter2004p84_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter2004p84-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> To put down and prevent further rebellions the Normans constructed castles and fortifications in unprecedented numbers,<sup id="cite_ref-Chibnall11_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chibnall11-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> initially mostly on the <a href="/wiki/Motte-and-bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte-and-bailey">motte-and-bailey</a> pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> William and his barons also exercised tighter control over inheritance of property by widows and daughters, often forcing marriages to Normans.<sup id="cite_ref-Mastery89_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mastery89-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Some Norman lords used England as a launching point for attacks into <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman conquest of Wales">spreading up the valleys</a> to create new <a href="/wiki/Marcher_Lord" title="Marcher Lord">Marcher</a> territories.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> By the time of William\'s death in 1087, England formed the largest part of an Anglo-Norman empire, ruled over by a network of nobles with landholdings across England, Normandy, and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> England\'s growing wealth was critical in allowing the Norman kings to project power across the region, including funding campaigns along the frontiers of Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At Christmas 1085, William ordered the compilation of a survey of the landholdings held by himself and by his vassals throughout the kingdom, organised by counties, a work now known as the <a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a>. The listing for each county gives the holdings of each landholder, grouped by owners. The listings describe the holding, who owned the land before the Conquest, its value, what the tax assessment was, and usually the number of peasants, ploughs, and any other resources the holding had. Towns were listed separately. All the English counties south of the River Tees and <a href="/wiki/River_Ribble" title="River Ribble">River Ribble</a> are included, and the whole work seems to have been mostly completed by 1 August 1086, when the <i>Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</i> records that William received the results and that all the chief magnates swore the <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_Salisbury" title="Oath of Salisbury">Salisbury Oath</a>, a renewal of their oaths of allegiance.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="William_II_.281087.E2.80.931100.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="William_II_(1087\xe2\x80\x931100)">William II (1087\xe2\x80\x931100)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: William II (1087\xe2\x80\x931100)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg/220px-Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg/330px-Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg/440px-Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2202" data-file-height="1272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Seal_of_William_Rufus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Great Seal of William II, King of England</div></div></div>\n<p>At the death of William the Conqueror in 1087 his lands were divided into two parts. His Norman lands went to the eldest son <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert Curthose</a> and his English lands to the younger William Rufus. This presented a dilemma for those nobles who held land on both sides of the waterway of the English Channel, who decided to unite England and Normandy once more under one ruler. The pursuit of this aim led them to revolt against William in favour of Robert in the <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_1088" title="Rebellion of 1088">Rebellion of 1088</a>, under the leadership of the powerful Bishop <a href="/wiki/Odo_of_Bayeux" title="Odo of Bayeux">Odo of Bayeux</a>, who was a half-brother of William the Conqueror.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter129_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter129-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> As Robert failed to appear in England to rally his supporters, William won the support of the English lords with silver and promises of better government, and defeated the rebellion. In 1091 he invaded Normandy, crushing Robert\'s forces and forcing him to cede a portion of his lands. The two made up their differences and William agreed to help Robert recover lands lost to the King of France, notably <a href="/wiki/Maine_(province)" title="Maine (province)">Le Maine</a>. This plan was later abandoned, but William continued to pursue a ferociously warlike defence of his French possessions and interests, exemplified by his response to the attempt by <a href="/wiki/Elias_I_of_Maine" class="mw-redirect" title="Elias I of Maine">Elias de la Fl\xc3\xa8che</a>, <a href="/wiki/Count_of_Maine" class="mw-redirect" title="Count of Maine">Count of Maine</a>, to take <a href="/wiki/Le_Mans" title="Le Mans">Le Mans</a> in 1099. William came into conflict with <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a>, Archbishop of Canterbury over <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian reforms">Gregorian reforms</a> in the Church. Eventually Anselm went into exile and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a>, involved in a major conflict with the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a>, came to a <a href="/wiki/Concordat" title="Concordat">concordat</a> with William, whereby William recognised Urban as pope, and Urban gave sanction to the Anglo-Norman ecclesiastical <i>status quo</i>. Anselm remained in exile, and William was able to claim the revenues of the archbishop of Canterbury to the end of his reign.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> William died while hunting in 1100.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Henry_I_.281100.E2.80.9335.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_I_(1100\xe2\x80\x9335)">Henry I (1100\xe2\x80\x9335)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Henry I (1100\xe2\x80\x9335)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteShipSinking.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Picture of White Ship" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/WhiteShipSinking.jpg/220px-WhiteShipSinking.jpg" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/45/WhiteShipSinking.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WhiteShipSinking.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early fourteenth-century depiction of the sinking of the <i><a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a></i> on 25 November 1120</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite Robert\'s rival claims, his younger brother Henry immediately seized power in England.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Robert, who invaded in 1101, disputed Henry\'s control of England. This military campaign ended in a negotiated settlement that confirmed Henry as king. The peace was short-lived, and Henry invaded the Duchy of Normandy in 1105 and 1106, finally defeating Robert at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tinchebray" title="Battle of Tinchebray">Battle of Tinchebray</a>. Henry kept Robert imprisoned for the rest of his life. Henry\'s control of Normandy was challenged by <a href="/wiki/Louis_VI_of_France" title="Louis VI of France">Louis VI of France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_VII,_Count_of_Flanders" title="Baldwin VII, Count of Flanders">Baldwin of Flanders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fulk,_King_of_Jerusalem" title="Fulk, King of Jerusalem">Fulk of Anjou</a>, who promoted the rival claims of Robert\'s son, <a href="/wiki/William_Clito" title="William Clito">William Clito</a>, and supported a major rebellion in the Duchy between 1116 and 1119. Following Henry\'s victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Br%C3%A9mule" title="Battle of Br\xc3\xa9mule">Battle of Br\xc3\xa9mule</a>, a favourable peace settlement was agreed with Louis in 1120.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Considered by contemporaries to be a harsh but effective ruler, Henry skilfully manipulated the barons in England and Normandy. In England, he drew on the existing Anglo-Saxon system of justice, local government and taxation, but also strengthened it with additional institutions, including the royal <a href="/wiki/Exchequer" title="Exchequer">exchequer</a> and itinerant <a href="/wiki/Judge" title="Judge">justices</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Normandy was also governed through a growing system of justices and an exchequer. Many of the officials that ran Henry\'s system were "new men", relatively low-born individuals who rose through the ranks as administrators.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Henry encouraged <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Reform" title="Gregorian Reform">ecclesiastical reform</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> but from 1101 he also became embroiled in a serious dispute  with Archbishop Anselm, which was resolved through a compromise solution in 1105.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He supported the <a href="/wiki/Cluniac" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniac">Cluniac</a> order and played a major role in the selection of the senior clergy in England and Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-Green2009P278_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Green2009P278-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Stephen.2C_Matilda_and_the_Anarchy_.281135.E2.80.9354.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stephen,_Matilda_and_the_Anarchy_(1135\xe2\x80\x9354)">Stephen, Matilda and the Anarchy (1135\xe2\x80\x9354)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stephen, Matilda and the Anarchy (1135\xe2\x80\x9354)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">The Anarchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen, King of England</a></div>\n<p>Henry\'s only legitimate son, <a href="/wiki/William_Adelin" title="William Adelin">William</a>, died aboard the <i><a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a></i> in the disaster of 1120, sparking a fresh succession crisis. Henry named his daughter <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Matilda</a> as his heir,<sup id="cite_ref-hooper_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hooper-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> but on Henry\'s death in 1135 her cousin <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen of Blois</a> had himself proclaimed king.<sup id="cite_ref-Schama2000p117_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama2000p117-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Matilda\'s husband <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Plantagenet,_Count_of_Anjou" title="Geoffrey Plantagenet, Count of Anjou">Geoffrey, Count of Anjou</a> showed little interest in England, but he supported Matilda by entering Normandy to claim her inheritance.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Matilda landed in England to challenge Stephen and was declared "Lady of the English" which resulted in a civil war called the Anarchy. Stephen was defeated and captured at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lincoln_(1141)" title="Battle of Lincoln (1141)">Battle of Lincoln (1141)</a> and Matilda was the effective ruler. When Matilda was forced to release Stephen in a hostage exchange for her half-brother <a href="/wiki/Robert,_1st_Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Robert, 1st Earl of Gloucester">Robert, 1st Earl of Gloucester</a>, Stephen was re-crowned. The conflict in England continued inconclusively. However, Geoffrey secured the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">Duchy of Normandy</a>. Matilda\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a>, by his marriage to <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">Eleanor of Aquitaine</a> had acquired the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">Duchy of Aquitaine</a> and was now immensely rich. With skilful negotiation with the war-weary barons of England and King Stephen, he agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Wallingford" title="Treaty of Wallingford">Treaty of Wallingford</a> and was recognised as Stephen\'s heir.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angevins">Angevins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Angevins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin kings of England</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Henry_II_.281154.E2.80.9389.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_II_(1154\xe2\x80\x9389)">Henry II (1154\xe2\x80\x9389)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Henry II (1154\xe2\x80\x9389)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg/220px-Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg/330px-Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="308" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eleonora_Jindra2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Twelfth-century depiction of <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Aquitaine" title="Eleanor of Aquitaine">Eleanor of Aquitaine</a> holding court</div></div></div>\n<p>After Stephen\'s death in 1154 Henry succeeded as the first Angevin king of England, so-called because he was also the <a href="/wiki/Counts_and_dukes_of_Anjou" title="Counts and dukes of Anjou">Count of Anjou</a> in Northern France, adding it to his extensive holdings in Normandy and Aquitaine.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter2004p191_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter2004p191-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> England became a key part of a loose-knit assemblage of lands spread across Western Europe, later termed the Angevin Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry asserted his authority over Brittany, even reorganising the Duchy into eight administrative districts and introducing Angevin legal reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> He pursued an aggressive policy in Wales, reclaiming lands lost by Anglo-Norman princes and conducting four punitive campaigns against Welsh princes that resulted in their submission to his authority. This underlined his overlordship, but he did not attempt a direct conquest. When the Scottish king <a href="/wiki/William_the_Lion" title="William the Lion">William the Lion</a> joined the rebellion of Henry\'s sons and was captured, it allowed Henry to extract homage from the Scottish king under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Falaise" title="Treaty of Falaise">Treaty of Falaise</a> (1174), which he did not pursue directly, but which would provide a justification for later interventions in Scottish kingship.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the mid-twelfth century Ireland was ruled by local <a href="/wiki/List_of_Irish_kings" title="List of Irish kings">kings</a>, although their authority was more limited than their counterparts in the rest of western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In the 1160s deposed King <a href="/wiki/Diarmait_Mac_Murchada" title="Diarmait Mac Murchada">Diarmait Mac Murchada</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Leinster" title="List of kings of Leinster">King of Leinster</a> turned to Henry for assistance in 1167, and the English king agreed to allow Diarmait to recruit mercenaries within his empire.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Diarmait put together a force of Anglo-Norman and Flemish mercenaries drawn from the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Marches" title="Welsh Marches">Welsh Marches</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Richard_de_Clare,_2nd_Earl_of_Pembroke" title="Richard de Clare, 2nd Earl of Pembroke">Richard de Clare</a>, known as Strongbow.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> With his new supporters, he reclaimed Leinster but died shortly afterwards in 1171; de Clare then claimed Leinster for himself.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Henry took this opportunity to intervene personally in Ireland, landing in October 1171.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s timing was influenced by several factors, including encouragement from Pope Alexander, who saw the opportunity to establish papal authority over the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Ireland" title="Christianity in Ireland">Irish church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s intervention was initially successful, with both the Irish and Anglo-Normans in the south and east of Ireland accepted his rule.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Windsor_(1175)" title="Treaty of Windsor (1175)">Treaty of Windsor</a> in 1175, under which <a href="/wiki/Ruaidr%C3%AD_Ua_Conchobair" title="Ruaidr\xc3\xad Ua Conchobair">Rory O\'Connor</a> would be recognised as the <a href="/wiki/High_king_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="High king of Ireland">high king of Ireland</a>, giving homage to Henry and maintaining stability on the ground on his behalf,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> meant that he had little direct control.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_II,_Plantagenet_Empire.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Henry_II%2C_Plantagenet_Empire.png/170px-Henry_II%2C_Plantagenet_Empire.png" width="170" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Henry_II%2C_Plantagenet_Empire.png/255px-Henry_II%2C_Plantagenet_Empire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/Henry_II%2C_Plantagenet_Empire.png 2x" data-file-width="322" data-file-height="376" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_II,_Plantagenet_Empire.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The extent of the Angevin Empire around 1172; solid yellow shows Angevin possessions, checked yellow Angevin hegemony</div></div></div>\n<p>Henry saw an opportunity to re-establish what he saw as his rights over the Church in England by reasserting the privileges held by Henry I when <a href="/wiki/Theobald_of_Bec" title="Theobald of Bec">Theobald, Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, died, by appointing his friend, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a> to the post. Henry had clashed with the church over whether bishops could excommunicate royal officials without his permission and whether he could try clerics without them appealing to Rome. However, Becket opposed Henry\'s <a href="/wiki/Constitutions_of_Clarendon" title="Constitutions of Clarendon">Constitutions of Clarendon</a> and fled into exile. Relations later improved, allowing Becket\'s return, but soon soured again when Becket saw the crowning as <a href="/wiki/Coregent" class="mw-redirect" title="Coregent">coregent</a> of Henry\'s son by the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a> as a challenge to his authority and excommunicated those who had offended him. On hearing the news Henry uttered the infamous phrase "What miserable drones and traitors have I nurtured and promoted in my household who let their lord be treated with such shameful contempt by a low born clerk". In response to please Henry three of his men murdered Becket in <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>, probably by misadventure after Becket resisted a botched arrest attempt.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In Christian Europe Henry was considered complicit in this crime, making him a pariah, and he was forced to make a dramatic exhibition of penance, publicly walking barefoot into the cathedral and allowing monks to scourge him.<sup id="cite_ref-Schama2000p117_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schama2000p117-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Henry II attempted to give his land-less youngest son, John, a wedding gift of three castles it prompted his three eldest sons and wife to rebel in the <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_1173%E2%80%931174" class="mw-redirect" title="Revolt of 1173\xe2\x80\x931174">Revolt of 1173\xe2\x80\x931174</a>. <a href="/wiki/Louis_VII_of_France" title="Louis VII of France">Louis VII</a> encouraged the three elder sons to destabilise his mightiest subject and not to wait for their inheritances. It was only after eighteen months of conflict that Henry II was able to force the rebels to submit to his authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p82_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p82-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In Le Mans in 1182 Henry II gathered his children to plan for <a href="/wiki/Partible_inheritance" title="Partible inheritance">partible inheritance</a> in which his eldest son, also called Henry, would inherit England, Normandy and Anjou; <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard</a> the Duchy of Aquitaine; <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_II,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Geoffrey II, Duke of Brittany">Geoffrey</a> Brittany and John would receive Ireland. This broke down into further conflict and <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Young_King" title="Henry the Young King">the younger Henry</a> rebelled again, but died of <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>. In 1186 Geoffrey died as a result of a tournament accident but Henry was still reluctant to have a sole heir<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p86_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p86-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  so, in 1189, Richard and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II of France</a> took advantage of a sickening Henry II with more success. Henry II was forced to accept humiliating peace terms, including naming Richard as sole heir. When Henry II died shortly afterwards his last words to Richard were allegedly "God grant that I may not die until I have my revenge on you".<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p109_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p109-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Richard_I_.281189.E2.80.9399.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Richard_I_(1189\xe2\x80\x9399)">Richard I (1189\xe2\x80\x9399)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Richard I (1189\xe2\x80\x9399)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg/220px-Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg/330px-Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg/440px-Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1724" data-file-height="970" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Church_of_Fontevraud_Abbey_Richard_I_effigy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The effigy of <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fontevraud_Abbey" title="Fontevraud Abbey">Fontevraud Abbey</a>, Anjou</div></div></div>\n<p>On the day of Richard\'s English coronation there was a mass slaughter of the Jews, described by <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Devizes" title="Richard of Devizes">Richard of Devizes</a> as a "holocaust".<sup id="cite_ref-Ackroyd2000_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ackroyd2000-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Quickly putting the affairs of the Angevin Empire in order he departed on Crusade to the Middle East in early 1190. In Sicily he came into conflict with <a href="/wiki/Tancred_of_Lecce" class="mw-redirect" title="Tancred of Lecce">Tancred I</a> over the rights of Richard\'s sister Queen <a href="/wiki/Joan_of_England,_Queen_consort_of_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="Joan of England, Queen consort of Sicily">Joan</a>, widow of the former king <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_Sicily" title="William II of Sicily">William II of Sicily</a>. Richard captured the city of <a href="/wiki/Messina,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Messina, Italy">Messina</a> on 4 October 1190 and using it to force Tancred into a peace agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> When his sister and his fianc\xc3\xa9e <a href="/wiki/Berengaria_of_Navarre" title="Berengaria of Navarre">Berengaria</a> along with several other ships, including the treasure ship were seized by the island\'s <a href="/wiki/Despot_(court_title)" title="Despot (court title)">despot</a> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Komnenos_of_Cyprus" title="Isaac Komnenos of Cyprus">Isaac Komnenos</a>, Richard conquered the island, which became a western feudal and Christian base in the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Opinions of Richard amongst his contemporaries were mixed. He had rejected and humiliated the king of France\'s sister; insulted and refused spoils of the <a href="/wiki/Third_crusade" class="mw-redirect" title="Third crusade">third crusade</a> to nobles like <a href="/wiki/Leopold_V,_Duke_of_Austria" title="Leopold V, Duke of Austria">Leopold V, Duke of Austria</a>, and was rumoured to have arranged the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Conrad_of_Montferrat" title="Conrad of Montferrat">Conrad of Montferrat</a>. His cruelty was demonstrated by his massacre of 2,600 prisoners in Acre.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p128_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p128-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> However, Richard was respected for his military leadership and courtly manners. He achieved victories in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a> but failed to capture Jerusalem, retreating from the Holy Land with a small band of followers.<sup id="cite_ref-rich_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rich-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Richard was captured by Leopold on his return journey in 1192. Custody was passed to <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Lion" title="Henry the Lion">Henry the Lion</a> and a tax of 25 percent of movables and income was required in England to pay the ransom of 100,000 marks, with a promise of 50,000 more, before Richard was released in 1194. In his absence Philip II of France had overrun much of Normandy, while <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">John of England</a> controlled much of the remainder of Richard\'s lands. On his return to England, Richard forgave John and re-established his control. Leaving England in 1194 never to return, Richard battled Phillip for the next five years for the return of the holdings seized during his incarceration. Close to total victory he was injured by an arrow during the siege of <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Ch%C3%A2lus-Chabrol" title="Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Ch\xc3\xa2lus-Chabrol">Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Ch\xc3\xa2lus-Chabrol</a> and died after lingering injured for ten days.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="John_.281199.E2.80.931216.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="John_(1199\xe2\x80\x931216)">John (1199\xe2\x80\x931216)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: John (1199\xe2\x80\x931216)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">John, King of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An illuminated picture of two armies of mounted knights fighting; the French side are on the left, the Imperial on the right." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg/220px-Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg/330px-Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg/440px-Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg 2x" data-file-width="606" data-file-height="579" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bataille_de_Bouvines.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The French victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bouvines" title="Battle of Bouvines">battle of Bouvines</a> doomed John\'s plan to retake Normandy in 1214 and led to the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons&#39; War">First Barons\' War</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Richard\'s failure in his duty to provide an heir caused a succession crisis. Anjou, Brittany, Maine and Touraine chose Richard\'s nephew and nominated heir, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_I,_Duke_of_Brittany" title="Arthur I, Duke of Brittany">Arthur</a>, while John succeeded in England and Normandy. Yet again Philip II of France took the opportunity to destabilise the Plantagenet territories on the European mainland, supporting his vassal Arthur\'s claim to the English crown. When Arthur\'s forces threatened his mother, John won a significant victory, capturing the entire rebel leadership at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mirebeau" title="Battle of Mirebeau">Battle of Mirebeau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Turnet1994p100_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turnet1994p100-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Arthur was murdered, it was rumoured by John\'s own hands, and his sister <a href="/wiki/Eleanor,_Fair_Maid_of_Brittany" title="Eleanor, Fair Maid of Brittany">Eleanor</a> would spend the rest of her life in captivity. John\'s behaviour drove numerous French barons to side with Phillip. The resulting rebellions by the Norman and Angevin barons broke John\'s control of the continental possessions, leading to the de facto end of the Angevin Empire, even though Henry III would maintain the claim until 1259.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones2012p161_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2012p161-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After re-establishing his authority in England, John planned to retake Normandy and Anjou. The strategy was to draw the French from Paris while another army, under <a href="/wiki/Otto_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto IV, Holy Roman Emperor</a>, attacked from the north. However, his allies were defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bouvines" title="Battle of Bouvines">Battle of Bouvines</a> in one of the most decisive and symbolic battles in French history.<sup id="cite_ref-Favier_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Favier-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The battle had both important and high-profile consequences.<sup id="cite_ref-Contramine_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contramine-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> John\'s nephew Otto retreated and was soon overthrown while King John agreed to a five-year truce. Philip\'s decisive victory was crucial in ordering politics in both England and France. The battle was instrumental in forming the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy_in_France" title="Absolute monarchy in France">absolute monarchy in France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smedley1836_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smedley1836-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>John\'s defeats in France weakened his position in England. The rebellion of his English vassals resulted in the treaty called <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, which limited royal power and established <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>. This would form the basis of every constitutional battle through the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> However, both the barons and the crown failed to abide by the terms of Magna Carta, leading to the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons&#39; War">First Barons\' War</a> in which the rebel barons invited an invasion by <a href="/wiki/Louis_VIII_of_France" title="Louis VIII of France">Prince Louis</a>. This is considered by some historians to mark the end of the Angevin period and the beginning of the Plantagenet dynasty with John\'s death and William Marshall\'s appointment as the protector of the nine-year-old <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Marshall won the war with victories at the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lincoln_(1217)" title="Battle of Lincoln (1217)">Lincoln</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dover_(1217)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Dover (1217)">Dover</a> in 1217, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lambeth" title="Treaty of Lambeth">Treaty of Lambeth</a> by which Louis renounced his claims.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones221_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones221-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In victory, the Marshal Protectorate reissued the <i>Magna Carta</i> agreement as a basis for future government.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_England#Late_medieval_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Social history of England">Social history of the High Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg/220px-Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg/330px-Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg/440px-Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1153" data-file-height="1176" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglo-Norman twelfth-century <a href="/wiki/Board_game" title="Board game">gaming piece</a>, illustrating soldiers presenting a sheep to a figure seated on a throne</div></div></div>\n<p>Within twenty years of the Norman conquest, the Anglo-Saxon elite had been replaced by a new class of Norman nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The new <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earls</a> (successors to the ealdermen), sheriffs and senior clergy were all drawn from their ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In many areas of society there was continuity, as the Normans adopted many of the Anglo-Saxon governmental institutions, including the tax system, mints and the centralisation of law-making and some judicial matters; initially sheriffs and the hundred courts continued to function as before.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The method of government after the conquest can be described as a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism_in_England" title="Feudalism in England">feudal system</a>, in that the new nobles held their lands on behalf of the king; in return for promising to provide military support and taking an oath of allegiance, called <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage</a>, they were granted lands termed a <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fief</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Honour_(feudal_land_tenure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Honour (feudal land tenure)">honour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Major nobles in turn granted lands to smaller landowners in return for homage and further military support, and eventually the peasantry held land in return for local labour services, creating a web of loyalties and resources enforced in part by new honorial courts.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> This system had been used in Normandy and concentrated more power in the king and the upper elite than the former Anglo-Saxon system of government.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The practice of slavery declined in the years after the conquest, as the Normans considered the practice backward and contrary to the teachings of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The more prosperous peasants, however, lost influence and power as the Normans made holding land more dependent on providing labour services to the local lord.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> They sank down the economic hierarchy, swelling the numbers of unfree <a href="/wiki/Villein" title="Villein">villeins</a> or <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>, forbidden to leave their manor or seek alternative employment.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the centre of power, the kings employed a succession of clergy as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">chancellors</a>, responsible for running the royal chancery, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Familia_regis" class="mw-redirect" title="Familia regis">familia regis</a></i>, the military household, emerged to act as a bodyguard and military staff.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> England\'s bishops continued to form an important part in local administration, alongside the nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Henry I and Henry II both implemented significant <a href="/wiki/Legal_reform" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal reform">legal reforms</a>, extending and widening the scope of centralised, royal law; by the 1180s, the basis for the future <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> had largely been established, with a standing law court in Westminster\xe2\x80\x94an early <a href="/wiki/Common_Bench" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Bench">Common Bench</a>\xe2\x80\x94and travelling judges conducting <a href="/wiki/Eyre_(legal_term)" title="Eyre (legal term)">eyres</a> around the country. King John extended the royal role in delivering justice, and the extent of appropriate royal intervention was one of the issues addressed in the <i>Magna Carta</i> of 1215.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many tensions existed within the system of government.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Royal landowning and wealth stretched across England, and placed the king in a privileged position above even the most powerful of the noble elite.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Successive kings, still needed more resources to pay for military campaigns, conduct building programmes or to reward their followers, and this meant exercising their feudal rights to interfere in the land-holdings of nobles.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> This was contentious and a frequent issue of complaint, as there was a growing belief that land should be held by hereditary right, not through the favour of the king.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Property and wealth became increasingly focused in the hands of a subset of the nobility, the great magnates, at the expense of the wider baronage, encouraging the breakdown of some aspects of local feudalism.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> As time went by, the Norman nobility intermarried with many of the great Anglo-Saxon families, and the links with the Duchy began to weaken.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> By the late twelfth century, mobilising the English barons to fight on the continent was proving difficult, and John\'s attempts to do so ended in civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_society">Women in society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Women in society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Women in the Middle Ages">Women in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_women" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon women">Anglo-Saxon women</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg/170px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg" width="170" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg/255px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg 2x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="506" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A depiction of an English woman c. 1170 using a <a href="/wiki/Spindle_(textiles)" title="Spindle (textiles)">spindle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distaff" title="Distaff">distaff</a>, while caring for a young child</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval England was a <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchal society</a> and the lives of women were heavily influenced by contemporary beliefs about gender and authority.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> However, the position of women varied according to factors including their <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a>; whether they were unmarried, married, widowed or remarried; and in which part of the country they lived.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Significant gender inequities persisted throughout the period, as women typically had more limited life-choices, access to employment and trade, and legal rights than men.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> After the Norman Conquest, the position of women in society changed. The rights and roles of women became more sharply defined, in part as a result of the development of the feudal system and the expansion of the English legal system; some women benefited from this, while others lost out.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The rights of widows were formally laid down in law by the end of the twelfth century, clarifying the right of free women to own property, but this did not necessarily prevent women from being <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forcibly remarried</a> against their wishes.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The growth of governmental institutions under a succession of bishops reduced the role of queens and their households in formal government. Married or widowed noblewomen remained significant cultural and religious patrons and played an important part in political and military events, even if chroniclers were uncertain if this was appropriate behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> As in earlier centuries, most women worked in agriculture, but here roles became more clearly gendered, with <a href="/wiki/Ploughing" class="mw-redirect" title="Ploughing">ploughing</a> and managing the fields defined as men\'s work, for example, and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">dairy production</a> becoming dominated by women.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_national_identity" title="English national identity">English national identity</a></div>\n<p>The Normans and French who arrived after the conquest saw themselves as different from the English. They had close family and economic links to the Duchy of Normandy, spoke <a href="/wiki/Norman_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman French">Norman French</a> and had their own distinctive culture.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> For many years, to be English was to be associated with military failure and serfdom.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> During the twelfth century, the divisions between the English and Normans began to dissolve as a result of intermarriage and cohabitation.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>  By the end of the twelfth century, and possibly as early as the 1150s, contemporary commentators believed the two peoples to be blending, and the loss of the Duchy in 1204 reinforced this trend.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The resulting society still prized wider French cultural values, however, and French remained the language of the court, business and international affairs, even if Parisians mocked the English for their poor pronunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> During the twelfth and thirteenth centuries, the English began to consider themselves superior to the Welsh, Scots and <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>. The English perceived themselves as civilised, economically prosperous and properly Christian, while the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_fringe" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic fringe">Celtic fringe</a> was considered lazy, barbarous and backward.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Following the invasion of Ireland in the late twelfth century, similar feelings were expressed about the Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Medieval_England" title="Religion in Medieval England">Religion in Medieval England</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecclesiastical_structures_and_orders">Ecclesiastical structures and orders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ecclesiastical structures and orders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photograph of a ruined abbey; a river passes by in the lower left hand of the picture, overhung with dark trees. A ruined abbey building in stone makes up the midground of the right side of the photograph." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg/240px-Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg/360px-Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg/480px-Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fountains_Abbey" title="Fountains Abbey">Fountains Abbey</a>, one of the new <a href="/wiki/Cistercians" title="Cistercians">Cistercian</a> monasteries built in the twelfth century</div></div></div>\n<p>The 1066 Norman conquest brought a new set of Norman and French churchmen to power;
