some adopted and embraced aspects of the former Anglo-Saxon religious system, while others introduced practices from Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Extensive English lands were granted to monasteries in Normandy, allowing them to create daughter priories and monastic cells across the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The monasteries were brought firmly into the web of feudal relations, with their holding of land linked to the provision of military support to the crown.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The Normans adopted the Anglo-Saxon model of monastic cathedral communities, and within seventy years the majority of English cathedrals were controlled by monks; every English cathedral, however, was rebuilt to some extent by the new rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> England\'s bishops remained powerful temporal figures, and in the early twelfth-century raised armies against Scottish invaders and built up extensive holdings of castles across the country.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>New orders began to be introduced into England. As ties to Normandy waned, the French <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniac order">Cluniac order</a> became fashionable and their houses were introduced in England.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a> spread quickly from the beginning of the twelfth century onwards, while later in the century the <a href="/wiki/Cistercians" title="Cistercians">Cistercians</a> reached England, creating houses with a more austere interpretation of the monastic rules and building the great abbeys of <a href="/wiki/Rievaulx_Abbey" title="Rievaulx Abbey">Rievaulx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fountains_Abbey" title="Fountains Abbey">Fountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> By 1215, there were over 600 monastic communities in England, but new endowments slowed during the thirteenth century, creating long-term financial problems for many institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The religious <a href="/wiki/Military_order_(society)" class="mw-redirect" title="Military order (society)">military orders</a> that became popular across Europe from the twelfth century onwards, including the <a href="/wiki/Templars" class="mw-redirect" title="Templars">Templars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hospitallers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitallers">Hospitallers</a>, acquired possessions in England.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_and_state">Church and state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Church and state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_and_state_in_medieval_Europe" title="Church and state in medieval Europe">Church and state in medieval Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg/170px-English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg/255px-English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg/340px-English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1298" data-file-height="1805" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mid-thirteenth-century depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Becket_controversy" title="Becket controversy">death of Archbishop Thomas Becket</a></div></div></div>\n<p>William the Conqueror acquired the support of the Church for the invasion of England by promising ecclesiastical reform.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> William promoted celibacy amongst the clergy and gave ecclesiastical courts more power, but also reduced the Church\'s direct links to Rome and made it more accountable to the king.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Tensions arose between these practices and the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Reforms">reforming movement</a> of Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Gregory VII</a>, which advocated greater autonomy from royal authority for the clergy, condemned the practice of <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a> and promoted greater influence for the papacy in church matters.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Despite the bishops continuing to play a major part in royal government, tensions emerged between the kings of England and key leaders within the English Church. Kings and archbishops clashed over rights of appointment and religious policy, and successive archbishops including <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theobald_of_Bec" title="Theobald of Bec">Theobald of Bec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Langton" title="Stephen Langton">Stephen Langton</a> were variously forced into exile, arrested by royal knights or even killed.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> By the early thirteenth century, however, the church had largely won its argument for independence, answering almost entirely to Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimages">Pilgrimages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pilgrimages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimages</a> were a popular religious practice throughout the Middle Ages in England, with the tradition dating back to the Roman period.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Typically pilgrims would travel short distances to a shrine or a particular church, either to do <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a> for a perceived sin, or to <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">seek relief</a> from an illness or other condition.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Some pilgrims travelled further, either to more distant sites within Britain or, in a few cases, onto the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Under the Normans, religious institutions with important shrines, such as <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Abbey" title="Glastonbury Abbey">Glastonbury</a>, Canterbury and <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Cathedral" title="Winchester Cathedral">Winchester</a>, promoted themselves as pilgrimage destinations, maximising the value of the historic miracles associated with the sites.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Accumulating <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> became an important task for ambitious institutions, as these were believed to hold curative powers and lent status to the site.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> By the twelfth century reports of posthumous <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a> by local saints were becoming increasing common in England, adding to the attractiveness of pilgrimages to prominent relics.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crusades">Crusades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Crusades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The idea of undertaking a pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> was not new in England, as the idea of religiously justified warfare went back to Anglo-Saxon times.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> While English participation in the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> between 1095\xe2\x80\x9399 was limited, England played a prominent part in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second</a>, <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">Fifth Crusades</a> over the next two centuries, with many crusaders leaving for <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">the Levant</a> during the intervening years.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Many of those who took up the Cross to go on a Crusade never actually left, often because the individual lacked sufficient funds to undertake the journey.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Raising funds to travel typically involved crusaders selling or mortgaging their lands and possessions, which affected their families and, at times, the economy as a whole was considerably affected.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_England" title="Geography of England">Geography of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Detail from a medieval illustrated manuscript, showing a bearded peasant in long red robes digging with a spade; a stylised tree makes up the right hand side of the image." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg/170px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg" width="170" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg/255px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg/340px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg 2x" data-file-width="402" data-file-height="757" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_digging.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An English <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serf</a> at work digging, c.&#160;1170</div></div></div>\n<p>England had a diverse geography in the medieval period, from the <a href="/wiki/Fenlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Fenlands">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> or the heavily wooded <a href="/wiki/Weald" title="Weald">Weald</a>, through to the upland <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moors</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, medieval England broadly formed two zones, roughly divided by the rivers <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">Exe</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">Tes</a>: the south and east of England had lighter, richer soils, able to support both <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoral</a> agriculture, while the poorer soils and colder climate [sic] of the north and west produced a predominantly pastoral economy.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Slightly more land was covered by trees than in the twentieth century, and <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_beaver" class="mw-redirect" title="European beaver">beavers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">wolves</a> lived wild in England, bears being hunted to extinction by the eleventh century and beavers by the twelfth.<sup id="cite_ref-Dyer2009p13_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dyer2009p13-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Of the 10,000 miles of roads that had been built by the Romans, many remained in use and four were of particular strategic importance\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Icknield_Way" title="Icknield Way">Icknield Way</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ermine_Street" title="Ermine Street">Ermine Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Watling_Street" title="Watling Street">Watling Street</a>\xe2\x80\x94which criss-crossed the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The road system was adequate for the needs of the period, although it was significantly cheaper to transport goods by water.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> The major river networks formed key transport routes, while many English towns formed navigable <a href="/wiki/Inland_port" title="Inland port">inland ports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For much of the Middle Ages, England\'s climate differed from that in the twenty-first century. Between the ninth and thirteenth centuries England went through the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>, a prolonged period of warmer temperatures; in the early thirteenth century, for example, summers were around 1&#160;\xc2\xb0C warmer than today and the climate was slightly drier.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> These warmer temperatures allowed poorer land to be brought into cultivation and for <a href="/wiki/Grapevine" class="mw-redirect" title="Grapevine">grapevines</a> to be cultivated relatively far north.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_demography">Economy and demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Economy and demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of England in the Middle Ages">Economy of England in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_England" title="Demography of England">Demography of England</a></div>\n<p>The English economy was fundamentally <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_English_Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of English Agriculture in the Middle Ages">agricultural</a>, depending on growing crops such as <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oats" class="mw-redirect" title="Oats">oats</a> on an <a href="/wiki/Open_field_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Open field system">open field system</a>, and husbanding <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Agricultural land became typically organised around <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">manors</a>, and was divided between some fields that the landowner would manage directly, called <a href="/wiki/Demesne" title="Demesne">demesne</a> land, and the majority of the fields that would be cultivated by local <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett,_p._313_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett,_p._313-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> These peasants would pay rent to the landowner either through agricultural labour on the lord\'s demesne fields or through rent in the form of cash and produce.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett,_p._313_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett,_p._313-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> By the eleventh century, a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> was flourishing across much of England, while the eastern and southern towns were heavily involved in <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Around 6,000 <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermills</a> were built to grind flour, freeing up labour for other more productive agricultural tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the Norman invasion caused some damage as soldiers looted the countryside and land was confiscated for castle building, the English economy was not greatly affected.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Taxes were increased, however, and the Normans established extensive <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forests</a> that were exploited for their natural resources and protected by <a href="/wiki/Royal_forest#Forest_law" title="Royal forest">royal laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The next two centuries saw huge growth in the English economy, driven in part by the increase in the population from around 1.5&#160;million in 1086 to between 4&#160;and 5&#160;million in 1300.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_1982a,_p._18_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_1982a,_p._18-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> More land, much of it at the expense of the royal forests, was brought into production to feed the growing population and to produce <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a> for export to Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-BaileyBartlettCantor_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BaileyBartlettCantor-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Many hundreds of new towns, some of them <a href="/wiki/Planned_community" title="Planned community">planned communities</a>, were built across England, supporting the creation of <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charter_fair" title="Charter fair">charter fairs</a> and other medieval institutions which governed the growing trade.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Jewish financiers played a significant role in funding the growing economy, along with the new Cistercian and Augustinian religious orders that emerged as major players in the wool trade of the north.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_English_Mining_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economics of English Mining in the Middle Ages">Mining</a> increased in England, with a <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> boom in the twelfth century helping to fuel the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(1154%E2%80%931485)" title="History of the English penny (1154\xe2\x80\x931485)">expansion of the money supply</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_warfare" title="Medieval warfare">Medieval warfare</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg/220px-BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg/330px-BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg/440px-BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="714" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BattleOf_Lincoln.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lincoln_(1141)" title="Battle of Lincoln (1141)">Battle of Lincoln (1141)</a> from the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Anglorum_(Henry_of_Huntingdon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Historia Anglorum (Henry of Huntingdon)">Historia Anglorum</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>Anglo-Norman warfare was characterised by <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attritional</a> military campaigns, in which commanders tried to raid enemy lands and seize castles in order to allow them to take control of their adversaries\' territory, ultimately winning slow but strategic victories. Pitched battles were occasionally fought between armies but these were considered risky engagements and usually avoided by prudent commanders.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradbury2009p71_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradbury2009p71-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The armies of the period comprised bodies of mounted, armoured <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a>, supported by <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">Crossbowmen</a> become more numerous in the twelfth century, alongside the older <a href="/wiki/Shortbow" class="mw-redirect" title="Shortbow">shortbow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradbury2009p74_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradbury2009p74-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> At the heart of these armies was the <i>familia regis</i>, the permanent military household of the king, which was supported in war by feudal levies, drawn up by local nobles for a limited period of service during a campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Mercenaries were increasingly employed, driving up the cost of warfare, and adequate supplies of ready cash became essential for the success of campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Naval forces played an important role during the Middle Ages, enabling the transportation of troops and supplies, raids into hostile territory and attacks on enemy fleets.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> English naval power became particularly important after the loss of Normandy in 1204, which turned the English Channel from a friendly transit route into a contested and critical border region.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although a small number of castles had been built in England during the 1050s, after the conquest the Normans began to build timber <a href="/wiki/Motte_and_bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte and bailey">motte and bailey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ringwork" title="Ringwork">ringwork</a> castles in large numbers to control their newly occupied territories.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> During the twelfth century the Normans began to build more castles in stone, with characteristic square <a href="/wiki/Keep" title="Keep">keeps</a> that supported both military and political functions.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Royal castles were used to control key towns and forests, whilst <a href="/wiki/Baron" title="Baron">baronial</a> castles were used by the Norman lords to control their widespread estates; a feudal system called the castle-guard was sometimes used to provide garrisons.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Castles and <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">sieges</a> continued to grow in military sophistication during the twelfth century.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">Medieval art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardham_church,_interior.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Hardham_church%2C_interior.jpg/300px-Hardham_church%2C_interior.jpg" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Hardham_church%2C_interior.jpg/450px-Hardham_church%2C_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Hardham_church%2C_interior.jpg/600px-Hardham_church%2C_interior.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2864" data-file-height="2148" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardham_church,_interior.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Romanesque paintings in <a href="/wiki/St_Botolph%27s_Church,_Hardham" title="St Botolph&#39;s Church, Hardham">St Botolph\'s Church, Hardham</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG/170px-Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG/255px-Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG/340px-Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1576" data-file-height="2072" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cath%C3%A9drale_Salisbury_clocher.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Salisbury_Cathedral" title="Salisbury Cathedral">Salisbury Cathedral</a> which (excluding the tower and spire) is in the <a href="/wiki/Early_English_Period" class="mw-redirect" title="Early English Period">Early English style</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Norman conquest introduced northern French artistic styles, particular in illuminated manuscripts and murals, and reduced the demand for carvings.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In other artistic areas, including embroidery, the Anglo-Saxon influence remained evident into the twelfth century, and the famous <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a> is an example of older styles being reemployed under the new regime.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">Stained glass</a> had been introduced into Anglo-Saxon England. Very few examples of glass survive from the Norman period, but there are a few examples that survive from minor monasteries and parish churches. The largest collections of twelfth-century stained glass at the Cathedrals of <a href="/wiki/York_Minster" title="York Minster">York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_music">Literature and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Literature and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_literature" title="Anglo-Norman literature">Anglo-Norman literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_literature" title="Middle English literature">Middle English literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_in_Medieval_England" title="Music in Medieval England">Music in Medieval England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_theatre" title="Medieval theatre">Medieval theatre</a></div>\n<p>Poetry and stories written in French were popular after the Norman conquest, and by the twelfth century some works on English history began to be produced in French verse.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Romantic poems about tournaments and courtly love became popular in Paris and this fashion spread into England in the form of <i>lays</i>; stories about the court of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> were also fashionable, due in part to the interest of Henry II.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> English continued to be used on a modest scale to write local religious works and some poems in the north of England, but most major works were produced in Latin or French.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Music and singing were important in England during the medieval period, being used in religious ceremonies, court occasions and to accompany theatrical works.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> From the eleventh century distinctive <a href="/wiki/Monophony" title="Monophony">monophonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Plainchant" class="mw-redirect" title="Plainchant">plainchant</a> was superseded, as elsewhere in Europe, by standardised <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_chant" title="Gregorian chant">Gregorian chant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a></div>\n<p>The Normans brought with them architectural styles from their own duchy, where austere stone churches were preferred. Under the early Norman kings this style was adapted to produce large, plain cathedrals with ribbed <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> During the twelfth century the Anglo-Norman style became richer and more ornate, with pointed arches derived from French architecture replacing the curved Romanesque designs; this style is termed <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">Early English Gothic</a> and continued, with variation, throughout the rest of the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> In domestic architecture, the Normans, having first occupied the older Anglo-Saxon dwellings, rapidly beginning to build larger buildings in stone and timber. The elite preferred houses with large, ground-floor halls but the less wealthy constructed simpler houses with the halls on the first floor; master and servants frequently lived in the same spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Wealthier town-houses were also built using stone, and incorporated business and domestic arrangements into a single functional design.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_representations">Popular representations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Popular representations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages_in_popular_culture" title="Middle Ages in popular culture">Middle Ages in popular culture</a></div>\n<p>The period has been used in a wide range of popular culture. <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s plays on the lives of the medieval kings have proved to have had long lasting appeal, heavily influencing both popular interpretations and histories of figures such as King John.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Other playwrights have since taken key events and personalities as the subject of drama, including <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Murder_in_the_Cathedral" title="Murder in the Cathedral">Murder in the Cathedral</a></i> (1935) and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Anouilh" title="Jean Anouilh">Jean Anouilh</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Becket" title="Becket">Becket</a></i> (1959), that focus on the death of Thomas Becket and <a href="/wiki/James_Goldman" title="James Goldman">James Goldman</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_in_Winter" title="The Lion in Winter">The Lion in Winter</a></i> (1966), which focuses on Henry II and his sons.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>\'s location of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> in the reign of Richard I and his emphasis on the conflict between Saxons and Normans set the template for much later fiction and film adaptations.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Historical_fiction" title="Historical fiction">Historical fiction</a> set in England during the Middle Ages remains persistently popular, with the 1980s and 1990s seeing a particular growth of historical <a href="/wiki/Detective_fiction" title="Detective fiction">detective fiction</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Peters" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellis Peters">Ellis Peters</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/The_Cadfael_Chronicles" title="The Cadfael Chronicles">The Cadfael Chronicles</a> set in the Anarchy,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> which is also the location of much of <a href="/wiki/Ken_Follett" title="Ken Follett">Ken Follett</a>\'s best-selling <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pillars_of_the_Earth" title="The Pillars of the Earth">The Pillars of the Earth</a></i> (1989).<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Film-makers have drawn extensively on the medieval period, often taking themes from Shakespeare or the Robin Hood ballads for inspiration and adapting historical romantic novels as <i><a href="/wiki/Ivanhoe" title="Ivanhoe">Ivanhoe</a></i> (1952).<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> More recent revivals of these genres include <i><a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood:_Prince_of_Thieves" title="Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves">Robin Hood: Prince of Thieves</a></i> (1991) and <i><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Heaven_(film)" title="Kingdom of Heaven (film)">Kingdom of Heaven</a></i> (2005).<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table
